<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Soy" redirects here. <p>The <b>soybean</b> (<i>Glycine max</i>), or <b>soya bean</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is a species of <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legume</a> native to <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, widely grown for its edible <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">bean</a>, which has numerous uses.
</p><p>Fat-free (defatted) soybean meal is a significant and cheap source of protein for animal feeds and many <a href="/wiki/TV_dinner" title="TV dinner">packaged meals</a>. For example, soybean products, such as <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> (TVP), are ingredients in many meat and <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a> substitutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riaz2006_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riaz2006-4">[4]</a></sup> The beans contain significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Phytic_acid" title="Phytic acid">phytic acid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_minerals" title="Dietary minerals">dietary minerals</a> and <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a>. <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">Soy vegetable oil</a>, used in food and industrial applications, is another product of processing the soybean crop. Traditional unfermented food uses of soybeans include <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>, from which <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tofu_skin" title="Tofu skin">tofu skin</a> are made. Fermented soy foods include <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermented_bean_paste" title="Fermented bean paste">fermented bean paste</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natto" title="Natto">natto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>.
</p><p>The main countries growing soybeans are the United States (32% of world total, 2016 forecast), Brazil (31%) and Argentina (18%).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Soy" originated as a corruption of the Chinese or Japanese names for <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">豉油</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">chǐyóu</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a>: <i><span lang="yue-jyutping">si6jau4</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Cantonese" title="Yale romanization of Cantonese">Cantonese Yale</a>: <i><span lang="yue">sihyàuh</span></i>) <span style="font-weight: normal"> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">醤油</span>, <i>shōyu</i>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The etymology of the genus, <i>Glycine</i>, comes from <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>. When naming the genus, Linnaeus observed that one of the species within the species had a sweet root. Based on the sweetness, the Greek word for sweet, glykós, was Latinized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">[5]</a></sup> The genus name is not related to the amino acid <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanvarieties.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="1790" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soybeanvarieties.jpg/170px-Soybeanvarieties.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soybeanvarieties.jpg/255px-Soybeanvarieties.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soybeanvarieties.jpg/340px-Soybeanvarieties.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanvarieties.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Varieties of soybeans are used for many purposes.</div></div></div>
<p>The genus <i>Glycine</i> Willd. is divided into two subgenera, <i>Glycine</i> and <i>Soja</i>. The subgenus <i>Soja</i> (Moench) F.J. Herm. includes the cultivated soybean, <i>Glycine max</i> (L.) Merr., and the wild soybean, <i><a href="/wiki/Glycine_soja" title="Glycine soja">Glycine soja</a></i> Sieb. &amp; Zucc. Both species are <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annuals</a>. <i>Glycine soja</i> is the wild ancestor of <i>Glycine max</i>, and grows wild in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Glycine_(plant)" title="Glycine (plant)">subgenus <i>Glycine</i></a> consists of at least 25 wild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a> species: for example, <i>Glycine canescens</i> F.J. Herm. and <i>G. tomentella</i> Hayata, both found in Australia and Papua New Guinea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Perennial soybean (<i>Neonotonia wightii</i>) originated in Africa and is now a widespread pasture crop in the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like some other crops of long domestication, the relationship of the modern soybean to wild-growing species can no longer be traced with any degree of certainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Cultigen" title="Cultigen">cultural variety</a> with a very large number of cultivars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like most plants, soybeans grow in distinct <a href="/wiki/Plant_morphology" title="Plant morphology">morphological</a> stages as they develop from seeds into fully mature plants.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germination">Germination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Germination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first stage of growth is <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>, a method which first becomes apparent as a seed's <a href="/wiki/Radicle" title="Radicle">radicle</a> emerges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-14">[14]</a></sup> This is the first stage of root growth and occurs within the first 48 hours under ideal growing conditions. The first <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> structures, the <a href="/wiki/Cotyledon" title="Cotyledon">cotyledons</a>, develop from the <a href="/wiki/Hypocotyl" title="Hypocotyl">hypocotyl</a>, the first plant structure to emerge from the soil. These cotyledons both act as leaves and as a source of nutrients for the immature plant, providing the seedling nutrition for its first 7 to 10 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glycine_max_003.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1292" data-file-width="1722" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/200px-Glycine_max_003.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/300px-Glycine_max_003.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/400px-Glycine_max_003.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glycine_max_003.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>soya bean fruit</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maturation">Maturation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Maturation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first true leaves develop as a pair of <a href="/wiki/Leaf#Divisions_of_the_blade" title="Leaf">single blades</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-14">[14]</a></sup> Subsequent to this first pair, mature <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">nodes</a> form compound leaves with three blades. Mature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trifoliolate" title="Trifoliolate">trifoliolate</a> leaves, having three to four <a href="/wiki/Leaflet_(botany)" title="Leaflet (botany)">leaflets</a> per leaf, are often between 6–15 cm (2.4–5.9 in) long and 2–7 cm (0.79–2.76 in) broad. Under ideal conditions, stem growth continues, producing new nodes every four days. Before flowering, roots can grow 1.9 cm (0.75 in) per day. If <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">rhizobia</a> are present, <a href="/wiki/Root_nodule" title="Root nodule">root nodulation</a> begins by the time the third node appears. Nodulation typically continues for 8 weeks before the <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia#Infection_and_signal_exchange" title="Rhizobia">symbiotic infection</a> process stabilizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-14">[14]</a></sup> The final characteristics of a soybean plant are variable, with factors such as genetics, soil quality, and climate affecting its form; however, fully mature soybean plants are generally between 51–127 cm (20–50 in) in height<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter19_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter19-15">[15]</a></sup> and have rooting depths between 76–152 cm (30–60 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowering">Flowering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Flowering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4096" data-file-width="2304" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg/120px-Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg/180px-Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg/240px-Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_soyabean_04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soybean flower buds</div></div></div>
<p>Flowering is <a href="/wiki/Photoperiodism" title="Photoperiodism">triggered by day length</a>, often beginning once days become shorter than 12.8 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-14">[14]</a></sup> This trait is highly variable however, with different <a href="/wiki/Variety_(botany)" title="Variety (botany)">varieties</a> reacting differently to changing day length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Soybeans form inconspicuous, self-fertile flowers which are borne in the <a href="/wiki/Leaf#Large-scale_features_.28leaf_morphology.29" title="Leaf">axil</a> of the leaf and are white, pink or purple. Depending of the soybean variety, node growth may cease once flowering begins. Strains that continue nodal development after flowering are termed "<a href="/wiki/Indeterminate_growth" title="Indeterminate growth">indeterminates</a>" and are best suited to climates with longer growing seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-14">[14]</a></sup> Often soybeans drop their leaves before the seeds are fully mature.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_flowers.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2000" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Soybean_flowers.png/120px-Soybean_flowers.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Soybean_flowers.png/180px-Soybean_flowers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Soybean_flowers.png/240px-Soybean_flowers.png 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_flowers.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Small, purple soybean flowers</div></div></div>
<p>The fruit is a hairy <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">pod</a> that grows in clusters of three to five, each pod is 3–8 cm (1.2–3.1 in) long and usually contains two to four (rarely more) <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a> 5–11 mm in diameter. Soybean seeds come in a wide variety sizes and <a href="/wiki/Husk" title="Husk">hull</a> colors such as black, brown, yellow, and green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter19_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter19-15">[15]</a></sup> Variegated and bicolored seed coats are also common.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seed_resilience">Seed resilience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Seed resilience">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4096" data-file-width="2304" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg/120px-Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg/180px-Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg/240px-Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_soyabean_fruits.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mature soybeans</div></div></div>
<p>The hull of the mature bean is hard, water-resistant, and protects the <a href="/wiki/Cotyledon" title="Cotyledon">cotyledon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hypocotyl" title="Hypocotyl">hypocotyl</a> (or "germ") from damage. If the seed coat is cracked, the seed will not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germinate" title="Germinate">germinate</a>. The scar, visible on the seed coat, is called the <a href="/wiki/Hilum_(biology)" title="Hilum (biology)">hilum</a> (colors include black, brown, buff, gray and yellow) and at one end of the hilum is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micropyle_(botany)" title="Micropyle (botany)">micropyle</a>, or small opening in the seed coat which can allow the absorption of water for sprouting.
</p><p>Remarkably, seeds such as soybeans containing very high levels of <a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">protein</a> can undergo <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a>, yet survive and revive after water absorption. <a href="/wiki/A._Carl_Leopold" title="A. Carl Leopold">A. Carl Leopold</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Aldo_Leopold" title="Aldo Leopold">Aldo Leopold</a>, began studying this capability at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boyce_Thompson_Institute_for_Plant_Research" title="Boyce Thompson Institute for Plant Research">Boyce Thompson Institute for Plant Research</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> in the mid-1980s. He found soybeans and corn to have a range of soluble <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> protecting the seed's cell viability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Patents were awarded to him in the early 1990s on techniques for protecting "biological membranes" and proteins in the dry state.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrogen-fixing_ability">Nitrogen-fixing ability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nitrogen-fixing ability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like many legumes, soybeans can <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">fix atmospheric nitrogen</a>, thanks to <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> bacteria from the <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">Rhizobia</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_composition">Chemical composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Chemical composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Together, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean oil</a> content account for 56% of dry soybeans by weight (36% protein and 20% <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>, table). The remainder consists of 30% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 9% water and 5% <a href="/wiki/Ash_(analytical_chemistry)" title="Ash (analytical chemistry)">ash</a> (table). Soybeans comprise approximately 8% seed coat or hull, 90% <a href="/wiki/Cotyledon" title="Cotyledon">cotyledons</a> and 2% <a href="/wiki/Hypocotyl" title="Hypocotyl">hypocotyl</a> axis or germ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<div style="float: right;">51%</div> 4.89 mg</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><b style="margin-left:-0.65em">Other constituents</b></th><td style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">
<b>Quantity</b></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;">Water</th><td style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">
8.54 g</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">Cholesterol</a></th><td style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">

</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<hr/><div class="wrap" style="font-size:90%;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a class="external text" href="http://ndb.nal.usda.gov/ndb/foods/show/4845?fgcd=&amp;manu=&amp;lfacet=&amp;format=Full&amp;count=&amp;max=35&amp;offset=&amp;sort=&amp;qlookup=16108" rel="nofollow">Link to Complete USDA Nutrient Database Entry</a></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<ul><li>Units</li>
<li>μg = <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> • mg = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milligram" title="Milligram">milligrams</a></li>
<li>IU = <a href="/wiki/International_unit" title="International unit">International units</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td class="wrap" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:90%;background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;">
<sup>†</sup>Percentages are roughly approximated using <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">US recommendations</a> for adults.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>100 grams of raw soybeans supply 446 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a> and are 9% water, 30% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 20% total <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and 36% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> (table).
</p><p>Soybeans are an exceptional source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" title="Essential nutrient">essential nutrients</a>, providing in a 100 gram serving (raw, for reference) high contents of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> (DV) especially for protein (36% DV), <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> (37%), <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (121%), <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> (120%), <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> (101%) and several <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Folate" title="Folate">folate</a> (94%) (table). High contents also exist for <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> (table).
</p><p>For human consumption, soybeans must be cooked with "wet" heat to destroy the <a href="/wiki/Trypsin_inhibitor" title="Trypsin inhibitor">trypsin inhibitors</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serine_protease_inhibitor" title="Serine protease inhibitor">serine protease inhibitors</a>). Raw soybeans, including the immature green form, are toxic to all <a href="/wiki/Monogastric" title="Monogastric">monogastric</a> animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protein">Protein</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Protein">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Complete_protein" title="Complete protein">Complete protein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein_quality" title="Protein quality">Protein quality</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">Soy protein</a></div>
<p>Most soy protein is a relatively heat-stable storage protein. This heat stability enables soy food products requiring high temperature cooking, such as <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> (soy flour) to be made.
</p><p>Soy is a good source of protein for vegetarians and <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegans</a> or for people who want to reduce the amount of meat they eat, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration_(United_States)" title="Food and Drug Administration (United States)">US Food and Drug Administration</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-chd_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-fda-chd-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Soy protein products can be good substitutes for animal products because, unlike some other beans, soy offers a 'complete' protein profile. ... Soy protein products can replace animal-based foods—which also have complete proteins but tend to contain more fat, especially saturated fat—without requiring major adjustments elsewhere in the diet.
</p></blockquote>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PDCAAS" title="PDCAAS">Protein Digestibility Corrected Amino Acid Score</a> (PDCAAS) of soy protein is the nutritional equivalent of meat, eggs, and <a href="/wiki/Casein" title="Casein">casein</a> for human growth and health. Soybean protein isolate has a <a href="/wiki/Biological_value" title="Biological value">biological value</a> of 74, whole soybeans 96, soybean milk 91, and eggs 97.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1991Report_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-1991Report-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">Soy protein</a> is essentially identical to the protein of other <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legume</a> seeds and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulses" title="Pulses">pulses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Moreover, soybeans can produce at least twice as much protein per acre than any other major vegetable or grain crop besides <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hempnut" title="Hempnut">hemp</a>, five to 10 times more protein per acre than land set aside for grazing animals to make milk, and up to 15 times more protein per acre than land set aside for meat production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSRL_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSRL-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graded_Seed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="4128" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Graded_Seed.jpg/200px-Graded_Seed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Graded_Seed.jpg/300px-Graded_Seed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Graded_Seed.jpg/400px-Graded_Seed.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graded_Seed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graded seed</div></div></div>
<p>All <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spermatophytes" title="Spermatophytes">spermatophytes</a>, except for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">grass</a>-<a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal</a> family, contain soybean-like 7S (vicilin) or 11S (legumin), (S denotes <a href="/wiki/Svedberg" title="Svedberg">Svedberg</a>, sedimentation coefficients) seed storage <a href="/wiki/Globulin" title="Globulin">globulin</a> proteins. Oats and rice are anomalous in that they also contain a majority of soybean-like protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seed_Proteins_1999_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seed_Proteins_1999-27">[27]</a></sup> Cocoa, for example, contains the 7S globulin, which contributes to cocoa/chocolate taste and aroma;,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> whereas coffee beans (coffee grounds) contain the 11S globulin responsible for coffee's aroma and flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vicilin and legumin proteins belong to the <a href="/wiki/Cupin_superfamily" title="Cupin superfamily">cupin superfamily</a>, a large family of functionally diverse proteins that have a common origin and whose evolution can be followed from bacteria to eukaryotes including animals and higher plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>2S <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albumins" title="Albumins">albumins</a> form a major group of homologous storage proteins in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dicot" title="Dicot">dicot</a> species and in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monocots" title="Monocots">monocots</a> but not in grasses (cereals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-34">[34]</a></sup> Soybeans contain a small but significant 2S storage protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> 2S albumin are grouped in the <a href="/wiki/Prolamin" title="Prolamin">prolamin</a> superfamily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Other allergenic proteins included in this 'superfamily' are the non-specific <a href="/wiki/Plant_lipid_transfer_proteins" title="Plant lipid transfer proteins">plant lipid transfer proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpha_amylase_inhibitor" title="Alpha amylase inhibitor">alpha amylase inhibitor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trypsin_inhibitors" title="Trypsin inhibitors">trypsin inhibitors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prolamin" title="Prolamin">prolamin</a> storage proteins of cereals and grasses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seed_Proteins_1999_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seed_Proteins_1999-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">Peanuts</a>, for instance, contain 20% 2S albumin but only 6% 7S globulin and 74% 11S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-34">[34]</a></sup> It is the high 2S albumin and low 7S globulin that is responsible for the relatively low lysine content of peanut protein compared to soy protein.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates">Carbohydrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The principal soluble <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> of mature soybeans are the disaccharide <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> (range 2.5–8.2%), the trisaccharide <a href="/wiki/Raffinose" title="Raffinose">raffinose</a> (0.1–1.0%) composed of one sucrose molecule connected to one molecule of <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>, and the tetrasaccharide <a href="/wiki/Stachyose" title="Stachyose">stachyose</a> (1.4 to 4.1%) composed of one sucrose connected to two molecules of galactose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharide" title="Oligosaccharide">oligosaccharides</a> raffinose and stachyose protect the viability of the soybean seed from desiccation (see above section on physical characteristics) they are not digestible sugars, so contribute to <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a> and abdominal discomfort in humans and other <a href="/wiki/Monogastric" title="Monogastric">monogastric</a> animals, comparable to the disaccharide <a href="/wiki/Trehalose" title="Trehalose">trehalose</a>. Undigested oligosaccharides are broken down in the intestine by native microbes, producing gases such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.
</p><p>Since soluble soy carbohydrates are found in the whey and are broken down during fermentation, soy concentrate, soy protein isolates, tofu, soy sauce, and sprouted soybeans are without flatus activity. On the other hand, there may be some beneficial effects to ingesting oligosaccharides such as <a href="/wiki/Raffinose" title="Raffinose">raffinose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stachyose" title="Stachyose">stachyose</a>, namely, encouraging indigenous <a href="/wiki/Bifidobacterium" title="Bifidobacterium">bifidobacteria</a> in the colon against putrefactive bacteria.
</p><p>The insoluble carbohydrates in soybeans consist of the complex polysaccharides <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemicellulose" title="Hemicellulose">hemicellulose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a>. The majority of soybean carbohydrates can be classed as belonging to <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fats">Fats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Raw soybeans are 20% fat, including <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> (3%), <a href="/wiki/Monounsaturated_fat" title="Monounsaturated fat">monounsaturated fat</a> (4%) and polyunsaturated fat, mainly as <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a> (table).
</p><p>Within <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean oil</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> portion of the seed is contained four <a href="/wiki/Phytosterol" title="Phytosterol">phytosterols</a>: <a href="/wiki/Stigmasterol" title="Stigmasterol">stigmasterol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sitosterol" title="Sitosterol">sitosterol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Campesterol" title="Campesterol">campesterol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brassicasterol" title="Brassicasterol">brassicasterol</a> accounting for about 2.5% of the lipid fraction; and which can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Steroid_hormone" title="Steroid hormone">steroid hormones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Additionally soybeans are a rich source of <a href="/wiki/Sphingolipid" title="Sphingolipid">sphingolipids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_other_major_staple_foods">Comparison to other major staple foods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Comparison to other major staple foods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following table shows the nutrient content of green soybean and other major staple foods, each in respective raw form. Raw soybeans, however, aren't edible and cannot be digested. These must be sprouted, or prepared and cooked for human consumption. In sprouted and cooked form, the relative nutritional and anti-nutritional contents of each of these grains is remarkably different from that of raw form of these grains reported in this table. The nutritional value of soybean and each cooked staple depends on the processing and the method of cooking: boiling, frying, roasting, baking, etc.
</p>

</caption>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cultivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AreialSeedingPlane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2790" data-file-width="1873" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AreialSeedingPlane.jpg/170px-AreialSeedingPlane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AreialSeedingPlane.jpg/255px-AreialSeedingPlane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AreialSeedingPlane.jpg/340px-AreialSeedingPlane.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AreialSeedingPlane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Biplane" title="Biplane">Biplane</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cropduster" title="Cropduster">cropduster</a> over US soybean field</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_soybean_diseases" title="List of soybean diseases">List of soybean diseases</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soybeans are a globally important crop, providing oil and protein. In the United States, the bulk of the harvest is solvent-extracted with <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>, and the "toasted" defatted soymeal (50% protein) then makes possible the raising of farm animals (e.g. chicken, hog, turkey) on a large industrial scale. Soybean products are used in a large variety of processed foods.
</p><p>During World War II, soybeans became important in both North America and Europe chiefly as substitutes for other protein foods and as a source of edible oil. During the war, the soybean was discovered as <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conditions">Conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
Cultivation is successful in climates with hot summers, with optimum growing conditions in mean temperatures of 20 to 30 °C (68 to 86 °F); temperatures of below 20 °C and over 40 °C (68 °F, 104 °F) stunt growth significantly. They can grow in a wide range of soils, with optimum growth in moist alluvial soils with a good organic content. Soybeans, like most legumes, perform <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a> by establishing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiotic" title="Symbiotic">symbiotic</a> relationship with the bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">Bradyrhizobium japonicum</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synonymy" title="Synonymy">syn.</a> <i>Rhizobium japonicum</i>; Jordan 1982). For best results, though, an inoculum of the correct strain of bacteria should be mixed with the soybean (or any legume) seed before planting. Modern crop <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> generally reach a height of around 1 m (3.3 ft), and take 80–120 days from sowing to harvesting.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg/200px-Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg/300px-Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg/400px-Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soya Fields in Argentina</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Environmental issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Environmental groups, such as <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">WWF</a>, have reported soybean cultivation (primarily as a feed crop for animals) and the probability of increased <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Brazil#Soybean_production" title="Deforestation in Brazil">soybean cultivation in Brazil</a> has destroyed huge areas of <a href="/wiki/Amazon_rainforest" title="Amazon rainforest">Amazon rainforest</a>, and is encouraging further <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>  However, note that the driving cause of this deforestation is the global demand for meat, which in turn demands huge amounts of land to grow feed crops for livestock.  According to the World Bank, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production" title="Environmental impact of meat production">animal agriculture</a> is responsible for up to 91% of the destruction of the Amazon rainforest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rainforestfoundation.org_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-rainforestfoundation.org-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pachamama.org_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pachamama.org-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onegreenplanet.org_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-onegreenplanet.org-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soils">Soils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Soils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soil scientists <a href="/wiki/Edson_Lobato" title="Edson Lobato">Edson Lobato</a> (Brazil), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrew_McClung" title="Andrew McClung">Andrew McClung</a> (U.S.), and <a href="/wiki/Alysson_Paolinelli" title="Alysson Paolinelli">Alysson Paolinelli</a> (Brazil) were awarded the 2006 <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Prize" title="World Food Prize">World Food Prize</a> for transforming the ecologically biodiverse savannah of the <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">Cerrado</a> region of Brazil into highly productive cropland that could grow profitable soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> However, even correcting for poor soils soybeans were an unlikely cash crop for the Cerrado. Soy did not fare well in the low latitudes. More than the heat and humidity, it was a lack of seasons that hampered production. In the higher more northerly latitudes, flowering coincides with the summer solstice, when the plants reach their maximum height. The first soybeans planted in the Cerrado, however, flowered early and, deprived of long summer days, remained stunted. For soy agriculture to take root in Mato Grosso it was first necessary to develop a "tropical soybean"—one that would flower later, giving the plants more time to fully mature. This was accomplished after years of crossbreeding by scientists within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embrapa" title="Embrapa">Embrapa</a>, the research arm of the Brazilian Ministry of Agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vqronline.org_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-vqronline.org-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_rust.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Soybean_rust.jpg/200px-Soybean_rust.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Soybean_rust.jpg/300px-Soybean_rust.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Soybean_rust.jpg/400px-Soybean_rust.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_rust.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soybean rust</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contamination_concerns">Contamination concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Contamination concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Human <a href="/wiki/Sewage_sludge" title="Sewage sludge">sewage sludge</a> can be used as fertilizer to grow soybeans. Soybeans grown in sewage sludge likely contain elevated concentrations of metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">Pests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soybean plants are vulnerable to a wide range of bacterial diseases, fungal diseases, viral diseases and parasites. One important pest is the <a href="/wiki/Helicoverpa_zea" title="Helicoverpa zea">corn earworm moth</a>, which is the most common and destructive pest of soybean growth in Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybean-Prod-history.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="640" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Soybean-Prod-history.png/300px-Soybean-Prod-history.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Soybean-Prod-history.png/450px-Soybean-Prod-history.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Soybean-Prod-history.png/600px-Soybean-Prod-history.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybean-Prod-history.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Soybean Production</b> (1961 - 2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><br/>Country code; <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166-1_alpha-3" title="ISO 3166-1 alpha-3">ISO 3166-1 alpha-3</a>, oth 86; other 86 countries. <br/>Top 8 countries produced 94.82% in 2016.</div></div></div>
<p>The global production of soybeans is forecast to be 337 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> in 2017–2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The United States, Brazil and Argentina are the world's largest soybean producers and represent more than 80% of global soybean production (table).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gsp_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-gsp-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the average worldwide yield for soybean crops was 2.8 tonnes per <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-62">[62]</a></sup> The three largest yields per hectare were in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, having an average nationwide soybean yield of 4.0 tonnes. The most productive soybean farms in the world in 2016 were in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, with a nationwide average farm yield of 4.3 tonnes per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960–1 Dillon round of the <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade" title="General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade">General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade</a> (GATT), the United States secured tariff-free access for its soybeans to the European market. In the 1960s, the United States exported over 90% of the world's soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> By 2005, the top soybean exporters were Argentina (39% of world soybean exports), United States (37%), and Brazil (16%), while the top importers were China (41% of world soybean imports), European Union (22%), Japan (6%) and Mexico (6%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Soybeans were a crucial crop in East Asia long before written records began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> There is evidence for soybean domestication between 7000 and 6600 BC in China, between 5000 and 3000 BC in Japan and 1000 BC in Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Prior to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermented</a> products such as fermented black soybeans (<i><a href="/wiki/Douchi" title="Douchi">douchi</a></i>), <i>jiang</i> (Chinese miso), <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natto" title="Natto">natto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a>, soy was considered sacred for its beneficial effects in <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, and it was eaten by itself, and as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bean_curd" title="Bean curd">bean curd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>.
</p><p>Soybeans were introduced to <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> in <a href="/wiki/Malay_Archipelago" title="Malay Archipelago">Malay Archipelago</a> circa 13th century or probably earlier. By the 17th century through their trade with Far East, soybeans and its products were traded by European traders (Portuguese, Spanish, and Dutch) in Asia, and supposedly reached Indian Subcontinent by this period.
</p><p>By the 18th century, soybeans were introduced to the Americas and Europe. Soy was introduced to Africa from China in the late 19th century, and is now widespread across the continent. They are now a major crop in the United States, Brazil, Argentina, India, and China.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The closest living relative of the soybean is <i><a href="/wiki/Glycine_soja" title="Glycine soja">Glycine soja</a></i> (previously called <i>G. ussuriensis</i>), a legume native to central China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-68">[68]</a></sup>
According to the ancient Chinese myth, in 2853 BC, the legendary <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Emperor Shennong</a> of China proclaimed that five plants were sacred: soybeans, rice, wheat, barley, and <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
Cultivation of soybeans took place over long periods of time in the prehistory of modern-day Japan, Korea and Northern China, based on archaeological evidence.
</p><p>The origin of soy bean cultivation remains scientifically debated. Early Chinese records mention that soybeans were a gift from the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_delta" title="Yangtze River delta">Yangtze River delta</a> and Southeast China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48-70">[70]</a></sup> Recent research, however, indicates that seeding of wild forms started early (before 5000 BC) in multiple locations throughout East Asia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOSONE_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLOSONE-71">[71]</a></sup> The Great Soviet Encyclopedia claims soybean cultivation originated in China about 5000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Some scholars suggest that soybean originated in China and was domesticated about 3500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siddiqi_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siddiqi-73">[73]</a></sup> However, the oldest preserved soybeans resembling modern varieties in size and shape were found in <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_site" title="Archaeological site">archaeological sites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> dated about 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stark_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-stark-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dating</a> of soybean samples recovered through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flotation_(archaeology)" title="Flotation (archaeology)">flotation</a> during excavations at the Early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mumun" title="Mumun">Mumun</a> period Okbang site in Korea indicated soybean was cultivated as a food crop in around 1000–900 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stark_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-stark-74">[74]</a></sup> Soybeans from the Jōmon period in Japan from 3000 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOSONE_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-PLOSONE-71">[71]</a></sup> are also significantly larger than wild varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOSONE_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-PLOSONE-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The cultivation of soybeans began in the eastern half of northern China by 2000 BC, but is almost certainly much older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy-76">[76]</a></sup> Soybeans became an important crop by the Zhou Dynasty (c. 1046 – 256 BC) in China. However, the details of where, when, and under what circumstances soybean developed a close relationship with people are poorly understood. Soy bean was unknown in South China before the Han period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOSONE_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-PLOSONE-71">[71]</a></sup> From about the first century AD to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> (15–16th centuries), soybeans were introduced into across South and Southeast Asia. This spread was due to the establishment of sea and land trade routes. The earliest Japanese textual reference to the soybean is in the classic <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> (Records of Ancient Matters), which was completed in 712 AD.
</p><p>Many people have claimed soybeans in Asia were historically only used after a fermentation process, which lowers the high <a href="/wiki/Phytoestrogen" title="Phytoestrogen">phytoestrogens</a> content found in the raw plant. However, terms similar to "<a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>" have been in use since 82 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> and there is evidence of <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a> consumption that dates to 220.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soybeans were mentioned as <i>kadêlê</i> (modern <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a> term: <i>kedelai</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> in an <a href="/wiki/Old_Javanese" title="Old Javanese">old Javanese</a> manuscript, Serat <a href="/wiki/Sri_Tanjung" title="Sri Tanjung">Sri Tanjung</a>, which dates around the 12th to 13th century <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historia_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Historia-80">[80]</a></sup> By the 13th century, the soybean had arrived and cultivated in Indonesia; it probably arrived much earlier however, carried by traders or merchants from Southern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known reference to it as "<a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>" appeared in 1815 in the <a href="/wiki/Serat_Centhini" title="Serat Centhini">Serat Centhini</a> manuscript.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The development of tempeh fermented soybean cake were probably took place earlier, circa 17th century in Java.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the 1600s, soy sauce spread from southern Japan across the region through the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> (VOC). The soybean probably arrived from southern China, moving southwest into northern parts of Indian Subcontinent by this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iberia">Iberia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Iberia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1603, "<a href="/wiki/Nippo_Jisho" title="Nippo Jisho">Vocabvlario da Lingoa de Iapam</a>", a famous Japanese-Portuguese dictionary, was compiled and published by Jesuit priests in Nagasaki. It contains short but clear definitions for about 20 words related to soyfoods - the first in any European language.
</p><p>The Luso-Hispanic traders were familiar with soybeans and soybean product through their trade with Far East since at least 17th century. However, it was not until the late 19th century that the first attempt to cultivate soybeans in the Iberian peninsula was undertaken. In 1880, the soybean was first cultivated in Portugal in the <a href="/wiki/Botanical_Garden_of_the_University_of_Coimbra" title="Botanical Garden of the University of Coimbra">Botanical Gardens at Coimbra</a> (Crespi 1935).
</p><p>In about 1910 in Spain the first attempts at Soybean cultivation were made by the Count of San Bernardo, who cultivated soybeans on his estates at Almillo (in southwest Spain) about 48 miles east-northeast of Seville.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soybeans were first introduced to North America from China in 1765, by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Bowen" title="Samuel Bowen">Samuel Bowen</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> sailor who had visited China in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/James_Flint_(merchant)" title="James Flint (merchant)">James Flint</a>, the first Englishman legally permitted by the Chinese authorities to learn Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The first "New World" soybean crop was grown on <a href="/wiki/Skidaway_Island,_Georgia" title="Skidaway Island, Georgia">Skidaway Island, Georgia</a> in 1765 by Henry Yonge from seeds given him by Samuel Bowen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Bowen grew soy near <a href="/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia" title="Savannah, Georgia">Savannah, Georgia</a>, possibly using funds from Flint, and made soy sauce for sale to England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coastalfields_Press_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coastalfields_Press-89">[89]</a></sup> Although, soybean was introduced into North America in 1765, for the next 155 years, the crop was grown primarily for <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">forage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1831, the first soy product "a few dozen India Soy" [sauce] arrived in Canada. Soybeans were probably first cultivated in Canada by 1855, and definitely in 1895 at Ontario Agricultural College.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was not until <a href="/wiki/Lafayette_Mendel" title="Lafayette Mendel">Lafayette Mendel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Burr_Osborne_(chemist)" title="Thomas Burr Osborne (chemist)">Thomas Burr Osborne</a> showed that the nutritional value of soybean seeds could be increased by cooking, moisture or heat, that soy went from a farm animal feed to a human food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>William Morse is considered the "father" of modern soybean agriculture in America. He and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Piper" title="Charles Piper">Charles Piper</a> (Dr. C. V. Piper) took what was an unknown Oriental peasant crop in 1910 and transformed it into a "golden bean" for America becoming one of America's largest farm crops and its most nutritious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to the 1920 in the USA, the soybean was mainly a forage crop, a source of oil, meal (for feed) and industrial products, with very little used as food. However, it took on an important role after World War I. During the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, the drought-stricken (<a href="/wiki/Dust_Bowl" title="Dust Bowl">Dust Bowl</a>) regions of the United States were able to use soy to regenerate their soil because of its nitrogen-fixing properties. Farms were increasing production to meet with government demands, and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> became a promoter of soybeans. He In 1931, Ford hired chemists <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyer_(chemist)" title="Robert Boyer (chemist)">Robert Boyer</a> and Frank Calvert to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_silk" title="Artificial silk">artificial silk</a>. They succeeded in making a textile fiber of spun soy protein fibers, hardened or tanned in a <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> bath, which was given the name <a href="/wiki/Azlon" title="Azlon">Azlon</a>. It never reached the commercial market. Soybean oil was used to <a href="/wiki/Soy_paint" title="Soy paint">paint</a> the automobiles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwarcz_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwarcz-97">[97]</a></sup> as well as fluid for shock absorbers.
</p><p>Tofu was introduced in Japanese American internment camps during World War II, and gradually spread into the mainstream cuisine. New varieties were developed to improve the blandness of soybean oil. The Counterculture in the San Francisco region popularize soy foods. Although practically unseen in 1900, by 2000 they covered upward of 70 million acres, second only to corn, and it became America's largest cash crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2018)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean_and_West_Indies">Caribbean and West Indies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Caribbean and West Indies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The soybean arrived in the Caribbean in the form of soy sauce made by Samuel Bowen in Savannah, Georgia, in 1767. It remains only a minor crop there, but its uses for human food are growing steadily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mediterranean_area">Mediterranean area</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Mediterranean area">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The soybean was first cultivated in Italy by 1760 in the Botanical Garden of Turin. During the 1780s it was grown in at least three other botanical gardens in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1935 soybeans are first introduced to Greece by Anton Brillmayer, an Austrian soybean breeder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> By 1939, soybeans have been cultivated in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>An entire book has been published on the history of soybeans and soyfoods in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wild soybeans were discovered in northeastern Australia in 1770 by explorers Banks and Solander. In 1804, the first soyfood product ("Fine India Soy" [sauce]) was sold in Sydney. In 1879, the first domesticated soybeans arrived in Australia, a gift of the Minister of the Interior Department, Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Europe">Western Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Western Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The soybean was first cultivated in France by 1779 (and perhaps as early as 1740). The two key early people and organizations introducing the soybean to France were the Society of Acclimatization (starting in 1855) and Li Yu-ying (from 1910). Li started a large tofu factory, where the first commercial soyfoods in France were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The soybean first arrived in Africa via Egypt in 1857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Europe">Central Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Central Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1873, Professor <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_J._Haberlandt" title="Friedrich J. Haberlandt">Friedrich J. Haberlandt</a> first became interested in soybeans when he obtained the seeds of 19 soybean varieties at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vienna_World_Exposition" title="Vienna World Exposition">Vienna World Exposition</a> (Wiener Weltausstellung). He cultivated these seeds in Vienna, and soon began to distribute them throughout Central and Western Europe. In 1875, he first grew the soybeans in Vienna, then in early 1876 he sent samples of seeds to seven cooperators in central Europe, who planted and tested the seeds in the spring of 1876, with good or fairly good results in each case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Most of the farmers who received seeds from him cultivated them, then reported their results back to him. Starting in Feb. 1876, he published these results first in various journal articles, and finally in his <i>magnum opus</i>, Die Sojabohne (The Soybean) in 1878. In northern Europe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lupin" title="Lupin">lupin</a>/lupine is known as the "soybean of the north"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a> manual from the 1930s promoted soy beans, which it called "Nazi beans" as an alternative to meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Asia">Central Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Central Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The soybean is first in cultivated Transcaucasia in Central Asia in 1876, by the Dungans. This region has never been important for soybean production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_America">Central America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Central America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first reliable reference to the soybean in this region dates from Mexico in 1877.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book-128_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-book-128-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The soybean first arrived in South America in Argentina in 1882.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Andrew McClung showed in the early 1950s that with soil amendments the <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">Cerrado</a> region of Brazil would grow soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The march of soybeans into deforested areas of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_rain_forest" title="Amazon rain forest">Amazon rain forest</a> would come later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vqronline.org_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-vqronline.org-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_modification">Genetic modification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Genetic modification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_soybean" title="Genetically modified soybean">Genetically modified soybean</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg/220px-Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg/330px-Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg/440px-Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different varieties of soybeans being grown together</div></div></div>
<p>Soybeans are one of the "<a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotech</a> food" crops that have been <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified</a>, and genetically modified soybeans are being used in an increasing number of products. In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Monsanto" title="Monsanto">Monsanto</a> company introduced <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">glyphosate-tolerant</a> soybeans that have been genetically modified to be resistant to Monsanto's <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosate</a> herbicides through substitution of the <i><a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium" title="Agrobacterium">Agrobacterium</a> sp.</i> (strain CP4) <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> EPSP (5-enolpyruvyl shikimic acid-3-phosphate) synthase. The substituted version is not sensitive to <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1997, about 8% of all soybeans cultivated for the commercial market in the United States were genetically modified. In 2010, the figure was 93%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> As with other glyphosate-tolerant crops, concern is expressed over damage to <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> A 2003 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> concluded the RR gene had been bred into so many different soybean cultivars, there had been little decline in genetic diversity, but "diversity was limited among elite lines from some companies".
</p><p>The widespread use of such types of GM soybeans in the Americas has caused problems with exports to some regions. GM crops require extensive certification before they can be legally imported into the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, where there is considerable supplier and consumer reluctance to use GM products for consumer or animal use. Difficulties with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-existence_of_genetically_modified_and_conventional_crops_and_derived_food_and_feed" title="Co-existence of genetically modified and conventional crops and derived food and feed">coexistence</a> and subsequent traces of cross-contamination of non-GM stocks have caused shipments to be rejected and have put a premium on non-GM soy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2006 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> report found the adoption of genetically engineered (GE) soy, corn and cotton reduced the amount of pesticides used overall, but did result in a slightly greater amount of <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a> used for soy specifically.
