The use of GE soy was also associated with greater <a href="/wiki/No-till_farming" title="No-till farming">conservation tillage</a>, indirectly leading to better soil conservation, as well as increased income from off-farming sources due to the greater ease with which the crops can be managed. Though the overall estimated benefits of the adoption of GE soybeans in the United States was $310 million, the majority of this benefit was experienced by the companies selling the seeds (40%), followed by biotechnology firms (28%) and farmers (20%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The patent on <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Glyphosate-tolerant&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Glyphosate-tolerant (page does not exist)">glyphosate-tolerant</a> soybeans expired in 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> so benefits can be expected to shift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, a team of American scientists announced they had sequenced the soybean genome – the first legume to be sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-5297531_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-5297531-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_2">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="689" data-file-width="723" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg/220px-TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg/330px-TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg/440px-TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tofu and soy sauce</div></div></div>
<p>Among the legumes, the soybean is valued for its high (38–45%) <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> content as well as its high (approximately 20%) oil content. Soybeans are the second-most valuable agricultural export in the United States, behind corn.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="No citation for export claim (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Approximately 85% of the world's soybean crop is processed into soybean meal and soybean oil, the remainder processed in other ways or eaten whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soybeans can be broadly classified as "vegetable" (garden) or field (oil) types. Vegetable types cook more easily, have a mild, nutty flavor, better texture, are larger in size, higher in protein, and lower in oil than field types. <b><a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">Tofu</a></b>, <b><a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a></b>, &amp; <b><a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a></b>, are among the top edible commodities made using soybeans. Producers prefer the higher protein cultivars bred from vegetable soybeans originally brought to the United States in the late 1930s. The "garden" cultivars are generally not suitable for mechanical combine harvesting because there is a tendency for the pods to shatter upon reaching maturity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soybean_oil">Soybean oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Soybean oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">Soybean oil</a></div>
<p>Soybean seed contains 18–19% oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-125">[125]</a></sup> To extract soybean oil from seed, the soybeans are cracked, adjusted for moisture content, rolled into flakes and solvent-extracted with commercial hexane. The oil is then refined, blended for different applications, and sometimes hydrogenated. Soybean oils, both liquid and partially hydrogenated, are exported abroad, sold as "vegetable oil", or end up in a wide variety of processed foods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soybean_meal">Soybean meal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Soybean meal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soybean_meal" title="Soybean meal">Soybean meal</a></div>
<p>Soybean meal, or soymeal, is the material remaining after solvent extraction of oil from soybean flakes, with a 50% <a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">soy protein</a> content. The meal is 'toasted' (a <a href="/wiki/Misnomer" title="Misnomer">misnomer</a> because the heat treatment is with moist steam) and ground in a <a href="/wiki/Hammer_mill" title="Hammer mill">hammer mill</a>. Ninety-seven percent of soybean meal production globally is used as livestock feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-125">[125]</a></sup> Soybean meal is also used in some <a href="/wiki/Dog_food" title="Dog food">dog foods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lusas_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lusas-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock_feed">Livestock feed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Livestock feed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the major uses of soybeans globally is as livestock feed, predominantly in the form of soybean meal. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernal_grass" title="Vernal grass">Spring grasses</a> are rich in omega-3 fatty acids, whereas soy is predominantly omega-6. The soybean hulls, which mainly consist of the outer coats of the beans removed before oil extraction, can also be fed to livestock, as well as whole soybean seeds after processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_for_human_consumption">Food for human consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Food for human consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tempeh_003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tempeh_003.jpg/220px-Tempeh_003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tempeh_003.jpg/330px-Tempeh_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tempeh_003.jpg/440px-Tempeh_003.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tempeh_003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">Tempeh</a>, fermented soybean cake</div></div></div>
<p>In addition to their use in livestock feed, soybean products are widely used for human consumption. Common soybean products include <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="#Meal">soy meal</a>, <a href="#Flour">soy flour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> (TVP), <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>, soy <a href="/wiki/Lecithin" title="Lecithin">lecithin</a> and <a href="#Oil">soybean oil</a>. Soybeans may also be eaten with minimal processing, for example in the Japanese food <i><a href="/wiki/Edamame" title="Edamame">edamame</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja">枝豆</span><span style="display:none">,</span> <i>edamame</i>)</span>, in which immature soybeans are boiled whole in their pods and served with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edible_salt" title="Edible salt">salt</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2368" data-file-width="4208" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg/220px-Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg/330px-Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg/440px-Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One type of soya chunk as meal maker</div></div></div>
<p>In China, Japan, and <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, soybean and soybean products are a common part of the diet. <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">Tofu</a> (豆腐 <i>dòufu</i>) is thought to have originated in China, along with <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a> and several varieties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybean_paste" title="Soybean paste">soybean paste</a> used as seasonings.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Japanese foods made from soya include <i><a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a></i> (味噌), <i><a href="/wiki/Natt%C5%8D" title="Nattō">nattō</a></i> (納豆), <i><a href="/wiki/Kinako" title="Kinako">kinako</a></i> (黄粉) and <i><a href="/wiki/Edamame" title="Edamame">edamame</a></i> (枝豆), as well as products made with tofu such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atsuage" title="Atsuage">atsuage</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aburaage" title="Aburaage">aburaage</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Korean_cuisine" title="Korean cuisine">Korean cuisine</a>, soybean sprouts (<a class="extiw" href="https://ko.wikipedia.org/wiki/%EC%BD%A9%EB%82%98%EB%AC%BC" title="ko:콩나물">콩나물</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kongnamul" title="Kongnamul">kongnamul</a></i>) are used in a variety of dishes, and are the base ingredient in <i><a href="/wiki/Doenjang" title="Doenjang">doenjang</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cheonggukjang" title="Cheonggukjang">cheonggukjang</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanjang" title="Kanjang"><i>ganjang</i></a>. In Vietnam, soybeans are used to make soybean paste (<i><a href="/wiki/T%C6%B0%C6%A1ng" title="Tương">tương</a></i>) in the North with the most popular products are <i>tương Bần</i>, <i>tương Nam Đàn</i>, <i>tương Cự Đà</i> as a garnish for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ph%E1%BB%9F" title="Phở">phở</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/G%E1%BB%8Fi_cu%E1%BB%91n" title="Gỏi cuốn">gỏi cuốn</a></i> dishes, as well as tofu (<i>đậu hũ</i> or <i>đậu phụ</i> or <i>tàu hũ</i>), soy sauce (<i>nước tương</i>), soy milk (<i>nước đậu</i> in the North or <i>sữa đậu nành</i> in the South), and <i>đậu hũ nước đường</i> (tofu sweet soup). In India, <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> is often called meal maker or soya chunks and is a popular substitute for meat.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flour">Flour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Flour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Soy_powder" title="commons:Category:Soy powder">Soybean flour</a></b></i>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1474" data-file-width="2000" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg/220px-JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg/330px-JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg/440px-JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Japanese soybean meat</div></div></div>
<p>Soy flour refers to soybeans ground finely enough to pass through a 100-mesh or smaller screen where special care was taken during desolventizing (not toasted) to minimize <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturation</a> of the protein to retain a high <a href="/wiki/Protein_dispersibility_index" title="Protein dispersibility index">protein dispersibility index</a>, for uses such as <a href="/wiki/Food_extrusion" title="Food extrusion">food extrusion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a>. It is the starting material for production of soy concentrate and soy protein isolate.
</p><p>Soy flour is made by roasting the soybean, removing the coat, and grinding into a flour. Soy flour is manufactured with different fat levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-129">[129]</a></sup> Alternatively, <b>raw</b> soy flour omits the roasting step.
</p>
<ul><li>Defatted soy flour is obtained from <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> <a href="/wiki/Extraction_(chemistry)" title="Extraction (chemistry)">extracted</a> flakes, and contains less than 1% oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-129">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Natural or full-fat soy flour is made from unextracted, dehulled beans, and contains about 18% to 20% oil."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-129">[129]</a></sup> Its high oil content requires the use of a specialized Alpine Fine Impact Mill to grind rather than the usual <a href="/wiki/Hammer_mill" title="Hammer mill">hammer mill</a>. Full-fat soy flour has a lower protein concentration than defatted flour. Extruded Full-Fat soy flour, ground in an Alpine mill, can replace/extend EGGS in baking and cooking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Full-fat soy flour is a component of the famous Cornell Bread recipe (think pizza)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></li>
<li>Low-fat soy flour is made by adding some oil back into defatted soy flour. Fat levels range from 4.5% to 9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_129-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-129">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>High-fat soy flour can also be produced by adding back soybean oil to defatted flour, usually at the level of 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy_lecithin" title="Soy lecithin">Soy lecithin</a> can be added (up to 15%) to soy flour to make lecithinated soy flour. It increases dispersibility and gives it emulsifying properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_129-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soy flour has 50% protein and 5% fiber. It has higher levels of protein, thiamine, riboflavin, phosphorus, calcium, and iron than <a href="/wiki/Wheat_flour" title="Wheat flour">wheat flour</a>. It does not contain <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_129-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-129">[129]</a></sup> As a result, <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>-raised <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">breads</a> made with soy flour are dense in texture. Among many uses, soy flour thickens sauces, prevents <a href="/wiki/Staling" title="Staling">staling</a> in baked food, and reduces oil absorption during frying. Baking food with soy flour gives it tenderness, moistness, a rich color, and a fine texture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_129-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soy grits are similar to soy flour except the soybeans have been toasted and cracked into coarse pieces.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Kinako" title="Kinako">Kinako</a></i> is a soy flour used in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a>.
</p><p><span style="font-size:90%;"><i>Section reference</i>: <a href="#CITEREFSmithCircle1972">Smith &amp; Circle (1972</a>, p. 442)</span>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soy-based_infant_formula">Soy-based infant formula<span id="Soy-based_infant_formula"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Soy-based infant formula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soy_milk_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="1840" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Soy_milk_I.jpg/170px-Soy_milk_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Soy_milk_I.jpg/255px-Soy_milk_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Soy_milk_I.jpg/340px-Soy_milk_I.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soy_milk_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Malaysian <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">Soy milk</a> carton, and a glass of the beverage</div></div></div>
<p>Soy-based <a href="/wiki/Infant_formula" title="Infant formula">infant formula</a> (SBIF) is sometimes given to infants who are not being strictly breastfed; it can be useful for infants who are either allergic to pasteurized cow milk proteins or who are being fed a <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegan</a> diet. It is sold in powdered, ready-to-feed, and concentrated liquid forms.
</p><p>Some reviews have expressed the opinion that more research is needed to determine what effect the phytoestrogens in soybeans may have on infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Diverse studies have concluded there are no adverse effects in human growth, development, or reproduction as a result of the consumption of soy-based infant formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutri_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nutri-139">[139]</a></sup> One of these studies, published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Nutrition" title="Journal of Nutrition">Journal of Nutrition</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutri_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nutri-139">[139]</a></sup> concludes that there are:
</p>
<blockquote><p>... no clinical concerns with respect to nutritional adequacy, sexual development, neurobehavioral development, immune development, or thyroid disease. SBIFs provide complete nutrition that adequately supports normal infant growth and development. FDA has accepted SBIFs as safe for use as the sole source of nutrition.</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Meat_and_dairy_alternatives_and_extenders">Meat and dairy alternatives and extenders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Meat and dairy alternatives and extenders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="640" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg/220px-Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg/330px-Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg/440px-Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Open package of a soy-based cream cheese alternative with chives</div></div></div>
<p>Soybeans can be processed to produce a texture and appearance similar to many other foods. For example, soybeans are the primary ingredient in many <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">dairy product</a> substitutes (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margarine" title="Margarine">margarine</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Soy_ice_cream&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Soy ice cream (page does not exist)">soy ice cream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_yogurt" title="Soy yogurt">soy yogurt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy_cheese" title="Soy cheese">soy cheese</a>, and soy cream cheese) and meat alternatives (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veggie_burgers" title="Veggie burgers">veggie burgers</a>). These substitutes are readily available in most supermarkets. Soy milk does not naturally contain significant amounts of digestible <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>. Many manufacturers of soy milk sell calcium-enriched products, as well. Soy is also used in <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>: the beans (sometimes mixed with grain) are fermented into a solid cake.
</p><p>Soy products also are used as a low-cost substitute in meat and poultry products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Food service, retail and institutional (primarily school lunch and correctional) facilities regularly use such "extended" products. Extension may result in diminished flavor, but fat and cholesterol are reduced. Vitamin and mineral fortification can be used to make soy products nutritionally equivalent to animal protein; the protein quality is already roughly equivalent. The soy-based meat substitute <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> has been used for more than 50 years as a way of inexpensively extending <a href="/wiki/Ground_beef" title="Ground beef">ground beef</a> without reducing its nutritional value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSRL_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSRL-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riaz2006_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riaz2006-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soy_nut_butter">Soy nut butter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Soy nut butter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The soybean is used to make a product called soy <a href="/wiki/Nut_butter" title="Nut butter">nut butter</a> which is similar in texture to peanut butter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweetened_soybean">Sweetened soybean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Sweetened soybean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sweet boiled beans are popular in Japan and Korea and the sweet boiled soybeans are called as "Daizu no <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nimame&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nimame (page does not exist)">Nimame</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E7%85%AE%E8%B1%86" title="ja:煮豆">ja</a>)</span>" in Japan and Kongjorim (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span lang="ko">콩조림</span>) in Korea. Sweet boiled beans are even used in sweetened buns, especially in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mame_Pan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mame Pan (page does not exist)">Mame Pan</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E8%B1%86%E3%83%91%E3%83%B3" title="ja:豆パン">ja</a>)</span>.
</p><p>The boiled and pasted edamame, called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zunda&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zunda (page does not exist)">Zunda</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E3%81%9A%E3%82%93%E3%81%A0" title="ja:ずんだ">ja</a>)</span>, is used as one of the <a href="/wiki/Sweet_bean_paste" title="Sweet bean paste">Sweet bean pastes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wagashi" title="Wagashi">Japanese confections</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coffee_substitute">Coffee substitute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Coffee substitute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Roasted and ground soybeans can be used as a caffeine-free substitute for <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>. After the soybeans are roasted and ground, they look similar to regular coffee beans or can be used as a powder similar to instant coffee, with aroma and flavor of roasted soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_products">Other products</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Other products">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soybeans with black hulls are used in Chinese fermented black beans, <i><a href="/wiki/Douchi" title="Douchi">douchi</a></i>, not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Black_turtle_bean" title="Black turtle bean">black turtle beans</a>.
</p><p>Soybeans are also used in industrial products, including oils, soap, cosmetics, <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resins</a>, plastics, inks, crayons, <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a>, and clothing. Soybean oil is the primary source of <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> in the United States, accounting for 80% of domestic biodiesel production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Soybeans have also been used since 2001 as fermenting stock in the manufacture of a brand of <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In 1936, Ford Motor Company developed a method where soybeans and fibers were rolled together producing a soup which was then pressed into various parts for their cars, from the distributor cap to knobs on the dash board. Ford also informed in public relation releases that in 1935 over five million acres (20,000 km<sup>2</sup>) was dedicated to growing soybeans in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PM_Apr_1936_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-PM_Apr_1936-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/American_Cancer_Society" title="American Cancer Society">American Cancer Society</a>, "Studies in humans have not shown harm from eating soy foods. Moderate consumption of soy foods appears safe for both breast cancer survivors and the general population, and may even lower breast cancer risk." There is insufficient research to indicate whether taking soy <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> has any effect on health or cancer risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-acs-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytochemicals">Phytochemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Phytochemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saponins" title="Saponins">Saponins</a>, a class of natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surfactants" title="Surfactants">surfactants</a> (soaps), are sterols that are present naturally in a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Whole_food" title="Whole food">plant foods</a>, including vegetables, legumes, and cereals such as oats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Whole soybeans contain from 0.17 to 6.16% saponins, 0.35 to 2.3% in defatted soy flour and 0.06 to 1.9% in tofu. Legumes such as soybean and chickpeas are the major source of saponins in the human diet. Sources of non-dietary saponins include alfalfa, sunflower, herbs and <a href="/wiki/Mexican_barbasco_trade" title="Mexican barbasco trade">barbasco</a>.
</p><p>Soy contains isoflavones like <a href="/wiki/Genistein" title="Genistein">genistein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daidzein" title="Daidzein">daidzein</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-151">[151]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Glycitein" title="Glycitein">glycitein</a>, an O-methylated isoflavone which accounts for 5–10% of the total isoflavones in soy food products. Glycitein is a <a href="/wiki/Phytoestrogen" title="Phytoestrogen">phytoestrogen</a> with weak estrogenic activity, comparable to that of the other soy isoflavones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Isoflavones">Isoflavones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Isoflavones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoflavones" title="Isoflavones">Isoflavones</a></div>
<p>Soy's content of isoflavones are as much as 3 mg/g dry weight. Isoflavones are <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenol</a> compounds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-151">[151]</a></sup> produced primarily by beans and other legumes, including peanuts and <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpeas</a>. Isoflavones are closely related to <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a> found in other plants, vegetables and flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soybeans contain the isoflavones, <a href="/wiki/Genistein" title="Genistein">genistein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daidzein" title="Daidzein">daidzein</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Phytoestrogen" title="Phytoestrogen">phytoestrogen</a> compounds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_151-3"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-151">[151]</a></sup> implicated as potentially beneficial factors in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" title="Cardiovascular diseases">cardiovascular diseases</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardio_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-cardio-153">[153]</a></sup> and numerous other conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_151-4"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-151">[151]</a></sup> No beneficial effects, however, have been shown in <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical research</a> to lower the risk of cardiovascular diseases, including high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_cholesterol" title="Blood cholesterol">blood cholesterol</a> levels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardio_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-cardio-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_infection" title="Respiratory infection">respiratory infections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-efsa_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-efsa-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cholesterol_and_heart_diseases">Cholesterol and heart diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Cholesterol and heart diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The dramatic increase in soyfood sales is largely credited to the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a>'s (FDA) approval of soy as a cholesterol-lowering food, along with other heart and health benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-chd_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-fda-chd-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 1995 review<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> concluded that soy protein is correlated with significant decreases in serum cholesterol, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Density_Lipoprotein" title="Low Density Lipoprotein">LDL</a> (bad cholesterol) and triglycerides. However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Density_Lipoprotein" title="High Density Lipoprotein">HDL</a> (good cholesterol) did not increase by a significant amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHRQ_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHRQ-157">[157]</a></sup> Soy phytoestrogens (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoflavones" title="Isoflavones">isoflavones</a>: genistein and daidzein) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adsorbed" title="Adsorbed">adsorbed</a> onto the soy protein were suggested as the agent reducing serum cholesterol levels.
</p><p>The FDA granted the following health claim for soy: "25 grams of soy protein a day, as part of a diet low in saturated fat and cholesterol, may reduce the risk of heart disease."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-chd_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-fda-chd-22">[22]</a></sup> One serving, (1 cup or 240 mL) of soy milk, for instance, contains 6 or 7 grams of soy protein. <a href="/wiki/Solae_(company)" title="Solae (company)">Solae</a> resubmitted their original petition, asking for a more vague health claim, after their original was challenged and highly criticized. Solae also submitted a petition for a health claim that soy can help prevent cancer. They quickly withdrew the petition for lack of evidence and after more than 1,000 letters of protest were received. On 18 February 2008, <a href="/wiki/Weston_A._Price_Foundation" title="Weston A. Price Foundation">Weston A. Price Foundation</a> submitted a petition for removal of this health claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-westonaprice_fda_petition_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-westonaprice_fda_petition-158">[158]</a></sup> 25 g/day soy protein was established as the threshold intake because most trials used at least this much protein and not because less than this amount is inefficacious. In fact, there is evidence suggesting that lower amounts are indeed efficacious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/American_Heart_Association" title="American Heart Association">American Heart Association</a> (AHA) review of a decade long study of soy protein benefits casts doubt on the FDA allowed "Heart Healthy" claim for soy protein and does not recommend isoflavone supplementation. The review panel also found that soy isoflavones have not been shown to reduce post-menopausal "hot flashes" and the efficacy and safety of isoflavones to help prevent cancers of the breast, uterus or prostate is in question. However, AHA concludes that "many soy products should be beneficial to cardiovascular and overall health because of their high content of polyunsaturated fats, fiber, vitamins, and minerals and low content of saturated fat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardio_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-cardio-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>The AHA did not conduct a formal statistical analysis of the 22 studies upon which they based their estimate of the potency of soy protein. When such an analysis was conducted, Jenkins et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> found that the AHA had considerably underestimated the <a href="/wiki/Hypocholesterolemia" title="Hypocholesterolemia">hypocholesterolemic</a> effects of soy protein. Further, when the analysis was limited to the 11 studies that provided evidence that the control and soy diets were matched, soy protein was found to lower LDL by 5.2 percent. This estimate is in line with the results of other recently published <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meta-analyses" title="Meta-analyses">meta-analyses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Furthermore, recent research suggests that soy protein decreases <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postprandial" title="Postprandial">postprandial</a> triglyceride levels, which is increasingly viewed as important for reducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_heart_disease" title="Coronary heart disease">coronary heart disease</a> risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytic_acid">Phytic acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Phytic acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phytic_acid" title="Phytic acid">Phytic acid</a></div>
<p>Soybeans contain a high level of <a href="/wiki/Phytic_acid" title="Phytic acid">phytic acid</a>, which has many effects including acting as an antioxidant and a <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelating agent</a>. The beneficial claims for phytic acid include reducing cancer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> minimizing diabetes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> and reducing inflammation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> However, phytic acid is also criticized for reducing vital minerals absorption due to its chelating effect, especially for diets already low in minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_risks">Health risks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Health risks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergy">Allergy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Allergy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soy_allergy" title="Soy allergy">Soy allergy</a></div>
<p>Allergy to soy is common, and the food is listed with other foods that commonly cause allergy, such as milk, eggs, peanuts, tree nuts, shellfish. The problem has been reported among younger children, and the diagnosis of soy allergy is often based on symptoms reported by parents and results of skin tests or blood tests for allergy. Only a few reported studies have attempted to confirm allergy to soy by direct challenge with the food under controlled conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> It is very difficult to give a reliable estimate of the true prevalence of soy allergy in the general population. To the extent that it does exist, soy allergy may cause cases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urticaria" title="Urticaria">urticaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">angioedema</a>, usually within minutes to hours of ingestion. In rare cases, true <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a> may also occur. The reason for the discrepancy is likely that soy proteins, the causative factor in <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergy</a>, are far less potent at triggering allergy symptoms than the proteins of peanut and shellfish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> An allergy test that is positive demonstrates that the immune system has formed IgE antibodies to soy proteins. However, this is only a factor when soy proteins reach the blood without being digested, in sufficient quantities to reach a threshold to provoke actual symptoms.
</p><p>Soy can also trigger symptoms via <a href="/wiki/Food_intolerance" title="Food intolerance">food intolerance</a>, a situation where no allergic mechanism can be proven. One scenario is seen in very young infants who have vomiting and <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhoea</a> when fed soy-based formula, which resolves when the formula is withdrawn. Older infants can suffer a more severe disorder with vomiting, diarrhoea that may be bloody, <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>, weight loss and failure to thrive. The most common cause of this unusual disorder is a sensitivity to cow's milk, but soy formulas can also be the trigger. The precise mechanism is unclear and it could be immunologic, although not through the IgE-type antibodies that have the leading role in urticaria and anaphylaxis. However, it is also self-limiting and will often disappear in the <a href="/wiki/Toddler" title="Toddler">toddler</a> years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytoestrogens">Phytoestrogens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Phytoestrogens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytoestrogens" title="Phytoestrogens">Phytoestrogens</a></div>
<p>Soybeans contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoflavones" title="Isoflavones">isoflavones</a> called <a href="/wiki/Genistein" title="Genistein">genistein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daidzein" title="Daidzein">daidzein</a>, which are one source of phytoestrogens in the human diet. Because most naturally occurring phytoestrogens act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_estrogen_receptor_modulators" title="Selective estrogen receptor modulators">selective estrogen receptor modulators</a>, or SERMs, which do not necessarily act as direct agonists of estrogen receptors, normal consumption of foods that contain these phytoestrogens should not provide sufficient amounts to elicit a physiological response in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11352776_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11352776-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lignans" title="Lignans">lignans</a> associated with high fiber foods such as cereal brans and beans are the principal precursor to mammalian lignans which have an ability to bind to human estrogen sites. Soybeans are a significant source of mammalian lignan precursor <a href="/wiki/Secoisolariciresinol" title="Secoisolariciresinol">secoisolariciresinol</a> containing 13–273 µg/100 g dry weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Another phytoestrogen in the human diet with estrogen activity is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coumestans" title="Coumestans">coumestans</a>, which are found in beans, split-peas, with the best sources being alfalfa, clover, and soybean sprouts. <a href="/wiki/Coumestrol" title="Coumestrol">Coumestrol</a>, an isoflavone <a href="/wiki/Coumarin" title="Coumarin">coumarin</a> derivative, is the only coumestan in foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soybeans and processed soy foods are among the richest foods in total phytoestrogens (wet basis per 100 g), which are present primarily in the form of the isoflavones daidzein and genistein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Human breast milk-containing or cow milk formula-containing diets provide 0.005-0.01 mg of isoflavone per day, while soy-based infant formulas provide  6–47 mg of isoflavone daily. This intake is several orders of magnitude greater than those provided by other nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Napier,_India_D._2014_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Napier,_India_D._2014-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breast_cancer">Breast cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Breast cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although considerable research has examined the potential for soy consumption to lower the risk of <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a> in women, assessment of these studies indicates there is insufficient population evidence to make a conclusion about this relationship as of 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_151-5"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2001 literature review suggested that women with current or past breast cancer should be aware of the risks of potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumor</a> growth when taking soy products, based on the effect of phytoestrogens to promote breast cancer cell growth in animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> A 2006 commentary reviewed the relationship with soy and breast cancer, stating that soy may decrease the risk of breast cancer, but cautioned that the impact of isoflavones on breast tissue needs to be evaluated at the cellular level in women at high risk for breast cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>A high consumption of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omega-6" title="Omega-6">omega-6</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fatty_acids" title="Polyunsaturated fatty acids">polyunsaturated fatty acids</a>, which are found in most types of <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oil</a> including soybean oil, may increase the likelihood that postmenopausal women will develop breast cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sonestedt_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sonestedt-181">[181]</a></sup> Another analysis suggests an inverse association between total polyunsaturated fatty acid intake and breast cancer risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2011 analysis of the literature said: "Our study suggests soy isoflavones intake is associated with a significant reduced risk of breast cancer incidence in Asian populations, but not in Western populations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prostate_cancer">Prostate cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Prostate cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of the phytoestrogen content, some studies have suggested that soybean ingestion may influence <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> levels in men. However, a 2010 meta-analysis of 15 placebo controlled studies showed that neither soy foods nor isoflavone supplements alter measures of bioavailable <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a> concentrations in men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> It has been hypothesized that soy foods and enterolactone may increase the development of <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a> although no significant associations were observed for the soy isoflavones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Furthermore, soy consumption has been shown to have no effect on the levels and quality of <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> A 2009 meta-analysis of the research on the association between soy consumption and prostate cancer risk in men concluded that "consumption of soy foods is associated with a reduction in prostate cancer risk in men."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brain">Brain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Brain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of mixed results from animal studies and epidemiological studies, a (relatively definitive but expensive) controlled study of the impacts of soy on cognitive skills was performed; it found no impact.
</p><p>Though there is some evidence that estrogen can help protect and repair the brain after injury in rats,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> there is also evidence that phytoestrogens may be harmful for the recovery of rats in other situations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> that have sustained brain injury.
</p><p>Similarly, epidemiological evidence of humans eating soya products is currently divided: a study of Japanese men between 1965 and 1999 demonstrated a positive correlation between brain atrophy and consumption of tofu meals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10763906_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10763906-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2001 <a href="/wiki/Literature_review" title="Literature review">literature review</a> noted that disturbing data on soy's effect on the cognitive function of the elderly existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> In 2008, an epidemiological study of 719 Indonesian elderly individuals found that tofu intake was associated with worse memory, but <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a> (a fermented soy product) intake was associated with better memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogervorst_E,_Sadjimim_T,_Yesufu_A,_Kreager_P,_Rahardjo_TB_2008_50–7_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hogervorst_E,_Sadjimim_T,_Yesufu_A,_Kreager_P,_Rahardjo_TB_2008_50–7-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cover article in the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Science_in_the_Public_Interest" title="Center for Science in the Public Interest">Center for Science in the Public Interest</a>'s September 2014 newsletter reported that a controlled study at USC prompted by suggestive epidemiological evidence found no impact on cognitive skills of years of soy vs milk protein diet enrichment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carcinogenicity">Carcinogenicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Carcinogenicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Though raw <a href="#Flour">soy flour</a> is known to be correlated with <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_cancer" title="Pancreatic cancer">pancreatic cancer</a> in rats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ToxSci_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-ToxSci-194">[194]</a></sup> the cooked flour has not been found <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogenic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CanRes_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-CanRes-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AdvExp_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-AdvExp-196">[196]</a></sup> Whether soy might promote pancreatic cancer in humans is unknown because studies have not yet attempted to single out soy intake and the incidence of pancreatic cancer in humans, and the amount of soy fed to the rats is proportionately far larger than what humans would normally consume. However, the soy isoflavone genistein has been suggested as a chemopreventive agent against pancreatic cancer, by interfering with the chemical pathways that promote the creation and growth of tumors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Cancer Council of <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>, Australia has released a statement saying scientific research suggests that overall the moderate consumption of soy products does not appear to present a risk to women with breast cancer, and there is equivocal evidence that consuming large amounts of soy products may have a protective effect against developing breast and prostate cancer. However, the Council does not recommend taking soy <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> as there is no evidence they are either effective or safe at preventing or treating cancers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thyroid_function">Thyroid function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Thyroid function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One review noted that soy based foods may inhibit absorption of thyroid hormone medications required for treatment of <a href="/wiki/Hypothyroidism" title="Hypothyroidism">hypothyroidism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> A 2015 scientific review by the <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> concluded that intake of isoflavones from supplements did not affect thyroid hormone levels in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postmenopausal" title="Postmenopausal">postmenopausal</a> women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gout">Gout</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Gout">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soybeans and soy products contain significant amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purines" title="Purines">purines</a>, a class of organic compounds. For people who suffer from <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a>, eating foods containing moderate or high levels of purines may make the condition worse. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">U.S. National Institutes of Health</a> (NIH) recommends that gout sufferers limit consumption of soy products (although also suggesting that soy may have health benefits by reducing the risk for heart disease).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> However, other researchers have found little or no association between consumption of purine-rich vegetables (including beans) and gout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Futures">Futures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Futures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Soybean <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures</a> are traded on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Board_of_Trade" title="Chicago Board of Trade">Chicago Board of Trade</a> and have delivery dates in January (F), March (H), May (K), July (N), August (Q), September (U), November (X).
</p><p>They are also traded on other commodity futures exchanges under different contract specifications:
</p>
<ul><li>SAFEX: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_South_African_Futures_Exchange" title="The South African Futures Exchange">The South African Futures Exchange</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></li>
<li>DC: <a href="/wiki/Dalian_Commodity_Exchange" title="Dalian Commodity Exchange">Dalian Commodity Exchange</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></li>
<li>KEX: <a href="/wiki/Kansai_Commodities_Exchange" title="Kansai Commodities Exchange">Kansai Commodities Exchange</a> in Japan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup></li>
<li>TGE: Tokyo Grain Exchange in Japan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup></li>
<li>KCX: Fukuoka Commodity Exchange in Japan that was absorbed by the KEX</li>
<li>NCDEX: National Commodity and Derivatives Exchange, India.</li>
<li>ROFEX: Rosario Grain Exchange in Argentina</li></ul>
